         </a>
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_16_crew.jpg">
          <img alt="Apollo 16 crew.jpg" data-file-height="1723" data-file-width="2391" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Apollo_16_crew.jpg/220px-Apollo_16_crew.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Apollo_16_crew.jpg/330px-Apollo_16_crew.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Apollo_16_crew.jpg/440px-Apollo_16_crew.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
         </a>
         <br/>
         <small>
          Left to right: Mattingly, Young, Duke
         </small>
        </p>
        <hr/>
        <table style="width: 100%;">
         <tr>
          <td colspan="2" style="text-align: center;">
           <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">
            Apollo program
           </a>
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td style="width: 50%;">
           
           <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">
            Apollo 15
           </a>
          </td>
          <td style="width: 50%; text-align: right;">
           <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">
            Apollo 17
           </a>
           
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      <b>
       Apollo 16
      </b>
      was the tenth manned mission in the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       United States
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">
       Apollo space program
      </a>
      , the fifth and penultimate to
      <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">
       land
      </a>
      on the
      <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
       Moon
      </a>
      and the first to land in the
      <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_the_Moon#The_highlands" title="Geology of the Moon">
       lunar highlands
      </a>
      . The second of the so-called "
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_mission_types" title="List of Apollo mission types">
       J missions
      </a>
      ," it was crewed by Commander
      <a href="/wiki/John_Young_(astronaut)" title="John Young (astronaut)">
       John Young
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
       Lunar Module Pilot
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Duke" title="Charles Duke">
       Charles Duke
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
       Command Module Pilot
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Ken_Mattingly" title="Ken Mattingly">
       Ken Mattingly
      </a>
      . Launched from the
      <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">
       Kennedy Space Center
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">
       Florida
      </a>
      at 12:54 PM EST on April 16, 1972, the mission lasted 11 days, 1 hour, and 51 minutes, and concluded at 2:45 PM EST on April 27.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-astronautix-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overvw_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-overvw-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      John Young and Charles Duke spent 71 hoursjust under three dayson the lunar surface, during which they conducted three
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity" title="Extra-vehicular activity">
       extra-vehicular activities
      </a>
      or moonwalks, totaling 20 hours and 14 minutes. The pair drove the
      <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Roving_Vehicle" title="Lunar Roving Vehicle">
       Lunar Roving Vehicle
      </a>
      (LRV), the second produced and used on the Moon, 26.7 kilometers (16.6mi). On the surface, Young and Duke collected 95.8 kilograms (211lb) of lunar samples for return to Earth, while Command Module Pilot Ken Mattingly orbited in the
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
       Command/Service Module
      </a>
      (CSM) above to perform observations. Mattingly spent 126 hours and 64 revolutions in lunar orbit. After Young and Duke rejoined Mattingly in lunar orbit, the crew released a
      <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">
       subsatellite
      </a>
      from the
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module#Service_Module_.28SM.29" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
       Service Module
      </a>
      (SM). During the return trip to Earth, Mattingly performed a one-hour spacewalk to retrieve several film cassettes from the exterior of the Service Module.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-astronautix-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overvw_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-overvw-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Apollo 16's landing spot in the highlands was chosen to allow the astronauts to gather geologically older lunar material than the samples obtained in the first four landings, which were in or near
      <a href="/wiki/Lunar_mare" title="Lunar mare">
       lunar maria
      </a>
      . Samples from the
      <a href="/wiki/Descartes_Highlands" title="Descartes Highlands">
       Descartes Formation
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Cayley_(crater)" title="Cayley (crater)">
       Cayley Formation
      </a>
      disproved a hypothesis that the formations were
      <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">
       volcanic
      </a>
      in origin.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Crew">
       Crew
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_16&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Crew">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="wikitable plainrowheaders">
      <tr>
       <th width="150">
        <a href="/wiki/Astronaut_ranks_and_positions" title="Astronaut ranks and positions">
         Position
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasmcrew_6-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-nasmcrew-6">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          6
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </th>
       <th colspan="2" scope="col" width="200">
        Astronaut
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <th scope="row">
        Commander
       </th>
       <td colspan="2">
        <a href="/wiki/John_Young_(astronaut)" title="John Young (astronaut)">
         John W. Young
        </a>
        <br/>
        Fourth spaceflight
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <th scope="row">
        Command Module Pilot
       </th>
       <td colspan="2">
        <a href="/wiki/Ken_Mattingly" title="Ken Mattingly">
         Thomas K. Mattingly II
        </a>
        <br/>
        First spaceflight
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <th scope="row">
        Lunar Module Pilot
       </th>
       <td colspan="2">
        <a href="/wiki/Charles_Duke" title="Charles Duke">
         Charles M. Duke, Jr.
        </a>
        <br/>
        Only spaceflight
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Mattingly had originally been assigned to the prime crew of
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">
       Apollo 13
      </a>
      , but was exposed to the
      <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">
       measles
      </a>
      through Duke, at that time on the back-up crew for Apollo 13, who had caught it from one of his children. He never contracted the illness, but was nevertheless removed from the crew and replaced by his backup,
      <a href="/wiki/Jack_Swigert" title="Jack Swigert">
       Jack Swigert
      </a>
      , three days before the launch.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Young, a
      <a href="/wiki/Captain_(United_States_O-6)" title="Captain (United States O-6)">
       captain
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">
       United States Navy
      </a>
      , had flown on three spaceflights prior to Apollo 16:
      <a href="/wiki/Gemini_3" title="Gemini 3">
       Gemini 3
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Gemini_10" title="Gemini 10">
       Gemini 10
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_10" title="Apollo 10">
       Apollo 10
      </a>
      , which orbited the Moon.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One of 19 astronauts selected by
      <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
       NASA
      </a>
      in April 1966, Duke had never flown in space before Apollo 16. He served on the support crew of Apollo 10 and was a
      <a href="/wiki/Flight_controller#Capsule_Communicator_.28CAPCOM.29" title="Flight controller">
       Capsule Communicator
      </a>
      (CAPCOM) for
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">
       Apollo 11
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Backup_crew">
       Backup crew
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_16&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Backup crew">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="wikitable plainrowheaders">
      <tr>
       <th width="150">
        <a href="/wiki/Astronaut_ranks_and_positions" title="Astronaut ranks and positions">
         Position
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasmcrew_6-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-nasmcrew-6">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          6
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </th>
       <th colspan="2" scope="col" width="200">
        Astronaut
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <th scope="row">
        Commander
       </th>
       <td colspan="2">
        <a href="/wiki/Fred_Haise" title="Fred Haise">
         Fred W. Haise, Jr.
        </a>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <th scope="row">
        Command Module Pilot
       </th>
       <td colspan="2">
        <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Roosa" title="Stuart Roosa">
         Stuart A. Roosa
        </a>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <th scope="row">
        Lunar Module Pilot
       </th>
       <td colspan="2">
        <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Mitchell" title="Edgar Mitchell">
         Edgar D. Mitchell
        </a>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Although not officially announced, the original backup crew consisted of
      <a href="/wiki/Fred_Haise" title="Fred Haise">
       Fred W. Haise
      </a>
      (CDR),
      <a href="/wiki/William_R._Pogue" title="William R. Pogue">
       William R. Pogue
      </a>
      (CMP) and
      <a href="/wiki/Gerald_P._Carr" title="Gerald P. Carr">
       Gerald P. Carr
      </a>
      (LMP), who were targeted for the prime crew assignment on
      <a href="/wiki/Canceled_Apollo_missions" title="Canceled Apollo missions">
       Apollo 19
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slayton_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Slayton-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, after the cancellations of Apollos
      <a href="/wiki/Canceled_Apollo_missions" title="Canceled Apollo missions">
       18
      </a>
      and 19 were finalized in September 1970 this crew would not rotate to a lunar mission as planned. Subsequently, Roosa and Mitchell were recycled to serve as members of the backup crew after returning from
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_14" title="Apollo 14">
       Apollo 14
      </a>
      , while Pogue and Carr were reassigned to the
      <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">
       Skylab
      </a>
      program where they flew on
      <a href="/wiki/Skylab_4" title="Skylab 4">
       Skylab 4
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Support_crew">

      <span class="mw-editsection">

        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Anthony_W._England" title="Anthony W. England">
        Anthony W. England
       </a>

       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Karl_Gordon_Henize" title="Karl Gordon Henize">

       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supportcrew_15-0">

         </span>
         15
         <span>

         </span>

       </sup>
      </li>

       <a href="/wiki/Henry_Hartsfield" title="Henry Hartsfield">

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_insignia">
       Mission insignia
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_16&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Mission insignia">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The insignia of Apollo 16 is dominated by a rendering of an
      <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">
       American eagle
      </a>
      and a red, white and blue shield, representing the people of the United States, over a gray background representing the lunar surface. Overlaying the shield is a gold NASA vector, orbiting the Moon. On its gold-outlined blue border, there are 16 stars, representing the mission number, and the names of the crew members: Young, Mattingly, Duke.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-insignia1_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-insignia1-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The insignia was designed from ideas originally submitted by the crew of the mission.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-insignia2_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-insignia2-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Planning_and_training">
       Planning and training
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_16&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Planning and training">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Landing_site_selection">
       Landing site selection
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_16&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Landing site selection">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Apollo 16 was the second of the Apollo type J missions, featuring the use of the Lunar Roving Vehicle, increased scientific capability, and lunar surface stays of three days.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix_2-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-astronautix-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As Apollo 16 was the penultimate mission in the Apollo program and there was no new hardware or procedures to test on the lunar surface, the last two missions (the other being
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">
       Apollo 17
      </a>
      ) presented opportunities for astronauts to clear up some uncertainties in understanding the Moon's properties. Although previous Apollo expeditions, including Apollo 14 and
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">
       Apollo 15
      </a>
      , obtained samples of pre-
      <a href="/wiki/Lunar_mare" title="Lunar mare">
       mare
      </a>
      lunar material, before
      <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">
       lava
      </a>
      began to upwell from the Moon's interior and flood the low areas and basins, none had actually visited the lunar highlands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overview_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-overview-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Apollo 14 had visited and sampled a ridge of material that had been ejected by the impact that created the
      <a href="/wiki/Mare_Imbrium" title="Mare Imbrium">
       Mare Imbrium impact basin
      </a>
      . Likewise, Apollo 15 had also sampled material in the region of Imbrium, visiting the basin's edge. There remained the possibility, because the Apollo 14 and Apollo 15 landing sites were closely associated with the Imbrium basin, that different geologic processes were prevalent in areas of the lunar highlands far from Mare Imbrium. Several members of the scientific community remarked that the central lunar highlands resembled regions on Earth that were created by
      <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">
       volcanic
      </a>
      processes and hypothesized the same might be true on the Moon. They had hoped that scientific output from the Apollo 16 mission would provide an answer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overview_20-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-overview-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_16_landing_site_AS16-M-0440.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="299" data-file-width="405" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Apollo_16_landing_site_AS16-M-0440.jpg/300px-Apollo_16_landing_site_AS16-M-0440.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/be/Apollo_16_landing_site_AS16-M-0440.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/be/Apollo_16_landing_site_AS16-M-0440.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_16_landing_site_AS16-M-0440.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Location of the Apollo 16 landing site
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_16_landing_site_AS14-69-9535.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3900" data-file-width="3900" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Apollo_16_landing_site_AS14-69-9535.jpg/220px-Apollo_16_landing_site_AS14-69-9535.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Apollo_16_landing_site_AS14-69-9535.jpg/330px-Apollo_16_landing_site_AS14-69-9535.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Apollo_16_landing_site_AS14-69-9535.jpg/440px-Apollo_16_landing_site_AS14-69-9535.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_16_landing_site_AS14-69-9535.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Oblique closeup of the proposed Apollo 16 landing site as photographed by
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_14" title="Apollo 14">
         Apollo 14
        </a>
        from lunar orbit.
        <a href="/wiki/North_Ray_(crater)" title="North Ray (crater)">
         North Ray
        </a>
        crater is at left and
        <a href="/wiki/South_Ray_(crater)" title="South Ray (crater)">
         South Ray
        </a>
        crater is at right, with bright rays.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Two locations on the Moon were given primary consideration for exploration by the Apollo 16 expedition: the
      <a href="/wiki/Descartes_Highlands" title="Descartes Highlands">
       Descartes Highlands
      </a>
      region west of
      <a href="/wiki/Mare_Nectaris" title="Mare Nectaris">
       Mare Nectaris
      </a>
      and the crater
      <a href="/wiki/Alphonsus_(crater)" title="Alphonsus (crater)">
       Alphonsus
      </a>
      . At Descartes, the Cayley and Descartes formations were the primary areas of interest in that scientists suspected, based on telescopic and orbital imagery, that the terrain found there was formed by magma more
      <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">
       viscous
      </a>
      than that which formed the lunar maria. The Cayley Formation's age was approximated to be about the same as Mare Imbrium based on the local frequency of impact craters. The considerable distance between the Descartes site and previous Apollo landing sites would be beneficial for the network of geophysical instruments,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      portions of which were deployed on each Apollo expedition beginning with
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">
       Apollo 12
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      At the Alphonsus, three scientific objectives were determined to be of primary interest and paramount importance: the possibility of old, pre-Imbrium impact material from within the crater's wall, the composition of the crater's interior and the possibility of past volcanic activity on the floor of the crater at several smaller "dark halo" craters. Geologists feared, however, that samples obtained from the crater might have been contaminated by the Imbrium impact, thus preventing Apollo 16 from obtaining samples of pre-Imbrium material. There also remained the distinct possibility that this objective had already been satisfied by the Apollo 14 and Apollo 15 missions, as the Apollo 14 samples had not yet been completely analyzed and samples from Apollo 15 had not yet been obtained.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It was decided to target the Apollo 16 mission for the Descartes site. Following the decision, the Alphonsus site was considered the most likely candidate for Apollo 17, but was eventually rejected. With the assistance of orbital photography obtained on the Apollo 14 mission, the Descartes site was determined to be safe enough for a manned landing. The specific landing site was between two young impact craters,
      <a href="/wiki/North_Ray_(crater)" title="North Ray (crater)">
       North Ray
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/South_Ray_(crater)" title="South Ray (crater)">
       South Ray
      </a>
      craters  1,000 and 680m (3,280 and 2,230ft) in diameter, respectively  which provided "natural drill holes" which penetrated through the lunar
      <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
       regolith
      </a>
      at the site, thus leaving exposed
      <a href="/wiki/Bedrock" title="Bedrock">
       bedrock
      </a>
      that could be sampled by the crew.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_and_Duke_train_for_Apollo_16.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="522" data-file-width="800" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Young_and_Duke_train_for_Apollo_16.jpg/220px-Young_and_Duke_train_for_Apollo_16.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Young_and_Duke_train_for_Apollo_16.jpg/330px-Young_and_Duke_train_for_Apollo_16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Young_and_Duke_train_for_Apollo_16.jpg/440px-Young_and_Duke_train_for_Apollo_16.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_and_Duke_train_for_Apollo_16.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        John Young and Charles Duke train at the
        <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_Gorge" title="Rio Grande Gorge">
         Rio Grande Gorge
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">
         New Mexico
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      After selecting the landing site for Apollo 16, sampling the Descartes and Cayley formations, two geologic units of the lunar highlands, was determined by mission planners to be the primary sampling interest of the mission. It was these formations that the scientific community widely suspected were formed by lunar volcanism, but this hypothesis was proven incorrect by the composition of lunar samples from the mission.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Training">
       Training
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_16&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Training">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In preparation for their mission, the Apollo 16 astronauts participated in an extensive training program that included several field
      <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">
       geology
      </a>
      trips to introduce the astronauts to concepts and techniques they would use on the lunar surface. During these trips, the astronauts visited and provided scientific descriptions of geologic features they were likely to encounter.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-training1_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-training1-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-training2_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-training2-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geotrips_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-geotrips-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In July 1971, the Apollo 16 astronauts visited
      <a href="/wiki/Greater_Sudbury" title="Greater Sudbury">
       Sudbury
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">
       Ontario
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
       Canada
      </a>
      for geology training exercises, the first time U.S. astronauts did so. Geologists chose the area because of a 60mi (97km) wide crater created about 1.8 billion years ago by a large meteorite.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Age_of_the_Sudbury_Meteorite_Impact_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Age_of_the_Sudbury_Meteorite_Impact-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Sudbury Basin shows evidence of shatter cone geology familiarizing the Apollo crew with geologic evidence of a meteor impact. During the training exercises the astronauts did not wear
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo/Skylab_A7L" title="Apollo/Skylab A7L">
       space suits
      </a>
      , but carried radio equipment to converse with each other and a scientist-astronaut, practicing procedures they would use on the lunar surface.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sudburytraining_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sudburytraining-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In addition to field geology training, the astronauts also trained to use the space suits, adapt to the reduced lunar gravity, collect samples, maneuver in the Lunar Roving Vehicle, and land and recover after the mission. They also received survival training and preparation for other technical aspects of the mission.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-training3_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-training3-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_highlights">
       Mission highlights
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_16&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mission highlights">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_and_outbound_trip">
       Launch and outbound trip
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_16&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Launch and outbound trip">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_16_Launch_-_GPN-2000-000638.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3000" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Apollo_16_Launch_-_GPN-2000-000638.jpg/220px-Apollo_16_Launch_-_GPN-2000-000638.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Apollo_16_Launch_-_GPN-2000-000638.jpg/330px-Apollo_16_Launch_-_GPN-2000-000638.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Apollo_16_Launch_-_GPN-2000-000638.jpg/440px-Apollo_16_Launch_-_GPN-2000-000638.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_16_Launch_-_GPN-2000-000638.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Apollo 16 launches from the
        <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">
         Kennedy Space Center
        </a>
        on April 16, 1972
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The launch of Apollo 16 was delayed one month from March 17 to April 16. This was the first launch delay in the Apollo program due to a technical problem. During the delay, the space suits, a
      <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">
       spacecraft
      </a>
      separation mechanism and batteries in the
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
       Lunar Module
      </a>
      (LM) were modified and tested.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-postponed1_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-postponed1-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There were concerns that the explosive mechanism designed to separate the docking ring from the
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
       Command Module
      </a>
      (CM) would not create enough pressure to completely sever the ring. This, along with a dexterity issue in Young's space suit and fluctuations in the capacity of the Lunar Module batteries, required investigation and trouble-shooting.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-postpone2_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-postpone2-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In January 1972, three months before the planned April launch date, a fuel tank in the Command Module was accidentally damaged during a routine test.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rocket was returned to the
      <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_Assembly_Building" title="Vehicle Assembly Building">
       Vertical Assembly Building
      </a>
      (VAB) and the fuel tank replaced, and the rocket returned to the launch pad in February in time for the scheduled launch.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The official mission countdown began on Monday, April 10, 1972, at 8:30 AM, six days before the launch. At this point the
      <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">
       Saturn V
      </a>
      rocket's three stages were powered up and drinking water was pumped into the spacecraft. As the countdown began, the crew of Apollo 16 was participating in final training exercises in anticipation of a launch on April 16. The astronauts underwent their final preflight physical examination on April 11.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On April 15,
      <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">
       liquid hydrogen
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">
       liquid oxygen
      </a>
      propellants were pumped into the spacecraft, while the astronauts rested in anticipation of their launch the next day.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Apollo 16 mission launched from the Kennedy Space Center in Florida at 12:54 PM EST on April 16, 1972.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-launch_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-launch-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The launch was nominal; the crew experienced vibration similar to that of previous crews. The first and second stages of the Saturn V rocket performed nominally; the spacecraft entered
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_earth_orbit" title="Low earth orbit">
       orbit
      </a>
      around Earth just under 12 minutes after lift-off. After reaching orbit, the crew spent time adapting to the zero-gravity environment and preparing the spacecraft for
      <a href="/wiki/Trans-lunar_injection" title="Trans-lunar injection">
       Trans Lunar Injection
      </a>
      (TLI), the burn of the third-stage rocket that would propel them to the Moon. In Earth orbit, the crew faced minor technical issues, including a potential problem with the environmental control system and the
      <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">
       S-IVB
      </a>
      third stage's attitude control system, but eventually resolved or compensated for them as they prepared to depart towards the Moon. After two orbits, the rocket's third stage reignited for just over five minutes, propelling the craft towards the Moon at about 22,000mph (35,000km/h).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Six minutes after the burn of the S-IVB, the Command/Service Module, containing the crew, separated from the rocket and traveled for 15m (49ft) before turning around and retrieving the Lunar Module from inside the expended rocket stage. The maneuver, known as transposition, went smoothly and the LM was extracted from the S-IVB.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fullsummary_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fullsummary-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following transposition and docking, the crew noticed the exterior surface of the Lunar Module was giving off particles from a spot where the LM's skin appeared torn or shredded; at one point, Duke estimated they were seeing about five to ten particles per second. The crew entered the Lunar Module through the docking tunnel connecting it with the Command Module to inspect its systems, at which time they did not spot any major issues. Once on course towards the Moon, the crew put the spacecraft into a rotisserie "barbecue" mode in which the craft rotated along its long axis three times per hour to ensure even heat distribution about the spacecraft from the Sun. After further preparing the craft for the voyage, the crew began the first sleep period of the mission just under 15 hours after launch.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ap_16_view_of_Earth_during_TLC.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1221" data-file-width="1233" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ap_16_view_of_Earth_during_TLC.jpg/220px-Ap_16_view_of_Earth_during_TLC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ap_16_view_of_Earth_during_TLC.jpg/330px-Ap_16_view_of_Earth_during_TLC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ap_16_view_of_Earth_during_TLC.jpg/440px-Ap_16_view_of_Earth_during_TLC.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ap_16_view_of_Earth_during_TLC.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Earth from Apollo 16 during the trans-lunar coast
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      By the time
      <a href="/wiki/Christopher_C._Kraft_Jr._Mission_Control_Center" title="Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center">
       Mission Control
      </a>
      issued the wake-up call to the crew for flight day two, the spacecraft was about 98,000 nautical miles (181,000km) away from the Earth, traveling at about 5,322ft/s (1,622m/s). As it was not due to arrive in lunar orbit until flight day four,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moonarrival_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-moonarrival-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      flight days two and three were largely preparatory days, consisting of spacecraft maintenance and scientific research. On day two, the crew performed an
      <a href="/wiki/Electrophoresis" title="Electrophoresis">
       electrophoresis
      </a>
      experiment, also performed on Apollo 14, in which they attempted to prove the higher purity of particle migrations in the zero-gravity environment. The remainder of day two included a two-second mid-course correction burn performed by the Command/Service Module's
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module#Service_Propulsion_System" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
       Service Propulsion System
      </a>
      engine to tweak the spacecraft's trajectory. Later in the day, the astronauts entered the Lunar Module for the second time in the mission to further inspect the landing craft's systems. The crew reported they had observed additional paint peeling from a portion of the LM's outer aluminum skin. Despite this, the crew discovered that the spacecraft's systems were performing nominally. Following the LM inspection, the crew reviewed checklists and procedures for the following days in anticipation of their arrival and the
      <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit" title="Lunar orbit">
       Lunar Orbit Insertion
      </a>
      burn. Command Module Pilot Mattingly reported a "
      <a href="/wiki/Gimbal_lock" title="Gimbal lock">
       gimbal lock
      </a>
      " warning light, indicating the craft was not reporting an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attitude_(aircraft)" title="Attitude (aircraft)">
       attitude
      </a>
      . Mattingly alleviated this by realigning the guidance system using the Sun and Moon. At the end of day two, Apollo 16 was about 140,000 nautical miles (260,000km) away from Earth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      At the beginning of day three, the spacecraft was about 157,000 nautical miles (291,000km) away from the Earth. The velocity of the craft steadily decreased, as it had not yet reached the lunar sphere of gravitational influence. The early part of day three was largely housekeeping, spacecraft maintenance and exchanging status reports with Mission Control in Houston. The crew performed the Apollo light flash experiment, or ALFMED, to investigate "light flashes" that were seen by the astronauts when the spacecraft was dark, regardless of whether or not their eyes were open, on Apollo lunar flights. This was thought to be caused by the penetration of the
      <a href="/wiki/Human_eye" title="Human eye">
       eye
      </a>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">
       cosmic ray
      </a>
      particles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alfmed_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-alfmed-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During the second half of the day, Young and Duke again entered the Lunar Module to power it up and check its systems, and perform housekeeping tasks in preparation for lunar landing. The systems were found to be functioning as expected. Following this, the crew donned their space suits and rehearsed procedures that would be used on landing day. Just before the end of flight day three at 59 hours, 19 minutes, 45 seconds after liftoff, while 178,673 nautical miles (330,902km) from the Earth and 33,821 nautical miles (62,636km) from the Moon, the spacecraft's velocity began increasing as it accelerated towards the Moon after entering the lunar sphere of influence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After waking up on flight day four, the crew began preparations for the maneuver that would brake the spacecraft into orbit around the Moon, or lunar orbit insertion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moonarrival_39-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-moonarrival-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At a distance of 11,142 nautical miles (20,635km) from the Moon, the
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Command/Service_Module#Service_Module_.28SM.29" title="Apollo Command/Service Module">
       Scientific Instrument Module
      </a>
      (SIM) bay cover was jettisoned. At just over 74 hours into the mission, the spacecraft passed behind the Moon, losing direct contact with Mission Control. While over the
      <a href="/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon" title="Far side of the Moon">
       far side of the Moon
      </a>
      , the Command/Service Module's Service Propulsion System engine burned for 6 minutes and 15 seconds, braking the spacecraft into an orbit around the Moon with a low point (pericynthion) of 58.3 and a high point (apocynthion) of 170.4 nautical miles (108.0 and 315.6km, respectively).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After entering lunar orbit, the crew began preparations for the Descent Orbit Insertion (DOI) maneuver to further modify the spacecraft's orbital trajectory. The maneuver was successful, decreasing the craft's pericynthion to 10.7 nautical miles (19.8km). The remainder of flight day four was spent making observations and preparing for activation of the Lunar Module, undocking, and landing the next day.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_surface">
       Lunar surface
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_16&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Lunar surface">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing,_Apollo_16.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2369" data-file-width="2340" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing%2C_Apollo_16.jpg/220px-Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing%2C_Apollo_16.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing%2C_Apollo_16.jpg/330px-Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing%2C_Apollo_16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing%2C_Apollo_16.jpg/440px-Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing%2C_Apollo_16.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing,_Apollo_16.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The lunar surface through a
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
         Lunar Module
        </a>
        window shortly after landing
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The crew continued preparing for Lunar Module activation and undocking shortly after waking up to begin flight day five. The boom that extended the
      <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">
       mass spectrometer
      </a>
      out from the Command/Service Module's Scientific Instruments Bay was stuck in a semi-deployed position. It was decided that Young and Duke would visually inspect the boom after undocking from the CSM in the LM. They entered the LM for activation and checkout of the spacecraft's systems. Despite entering the LM 40 minutes ahead of schedule, they completed preparations only 10 minutes early due to numerous delays in the process.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fullsummary_37-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-fullsummary-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With the preparations finished, they undocked in the LM
      <i>
       Orion
      </i>
      from Mattingly in the Command/Service Module
      <i>
       Casper
      </i>
      96 hours, 13 minutes, 13 seconds into the mission.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For the rest of the two crafts' passes over the
      <a href="/wiki/Near_side_of_the_Moon" title="Near side of the Moon">
       near side of the Moon
      </a>
      , Mattingly prepared to shift
      <i>
       Casper
      </i>
      to a circular orbit while Young and Duke prepared
      <i>
       Orion
      </i>
      for the descent to the lunar surface. At this point, during tests of the CSM's steerable rocket engine in preparation for the burn to modify the craft's orbit, a malfunction occurred in the engine's backup system. According to mission rules,
      <i>
       Orion
      </i>
      would have then re-docked with
      <i>
       Casper
      </i>
      , in case Mission Control decided to abort the landing and use the Lunar Module's engines for the return trip to Earth. After several hours of analysis, however, mission controllers determined that the malfunction could be worked around and Young and Duke could proceed with the landing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overview_20-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-overview-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As a result of this, powered descent to the lunar surface began about six hours behind schedule. Because of the delay, Young and Duke began their descent to the surface at an altitude higher than that of any previous mission, at 20.1 kilometers (10.9nmi). At an altitude of about 4,000m (13,000ft), Young was able to view the landing site in its entirety. Throttle-down of the LM's landing engine occurred on time and the spacecraft tilted forward to its landing orientation at an altitude of 2,200m (7,200ft). The LM landed 270m (890ft) north and 60m (200ft) west of the planned landing site at 104 hours, 29 minutes, and 35 seconds into the mission, at 2:23:35 UTC on April 21.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fullsummary_37-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-fullsummary-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After landing, Young and Duke began powering down some of the LM's systems to conserve battery power. Upon completing their initial adjustments, the pair configured
      <i>
       Orion
      </i>
      for their three-day stay on the lunar surface, removed their space suits and took initial geological observations of the immediate landing site. They then settled down for their first meal on the surface. After eating, they configured the cabin for their first sleep period on the Moon.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The landing delay caused by the malfunction in the Command/Service Module's main engine necessitated significant modifications to the mission schedule. Apollo 16 would spend one less day in lunar orbit after surface exploration had been completed to afford the crew contingency time to compensate for any further problems and to conserve expendables. In order to improve Young's and Duke's sleep schedule, the third and final moonwalk of the mission was trimmed from seven hours to five.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fullsummary_37-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-fullsummary-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_W._Young_on_the_Moon.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3928" data-file-width="3928" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/John_W._Young_on_the_Moon.jpg/220px-John_W._Young_on_the_Moon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/John_W._Young_on_the_Moon.jpg/330px-John_W._Young_on_the_Moon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/John_W._Young_on_the_Moon.jpg/440px-John_W._Young_on_the_Moon.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_W._Young_on_the_Moon.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        John Young - caught mid-leap - salutes the
        <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Flag_Assembly" title="Lunar Flag Assembly">
         American flag
        </a>
        on the lunar surface
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The next morning, flight day five, Young and Duke ate breakfast and began preparations for the first extra-vehicular activity (EVA), or moonwalk.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After the pair donned and pressurized their space suits and depressurized the Lunar Module cabin, Young climbed out onto the "porch" of the LM, a small platform above the ladder. Duke handed Young a jettison bag full of trash to dispose of on the surface.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-briar_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-briar-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Young then lowered the equipment transfer bag (ETB), containing equipment for use during the EVA, to the surface. Young descended the ladder and, upon setting foot on the lunar surface, became the ninth human to walk on the Moon.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fullsummary_37-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-fullsummary-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Upon stepping onto the surface, Young expressed his sentiments about being there: "There you are: Mysterious and Unknown Descartes. Highland plains. Apollo 16 is gonna change your image. I'm sure glad they got ol'
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brer_Rabbit" title="Brer Rabbit">
       Brer Rabbit
      </a>
      , here, back in the briar patch where he belongs."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-briar_52-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-briar-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Duke soon descended the ladder and joined Young on the surface, becoming the tenth and youngest human to walk on the Moon, at age 36. After setting foot on the lunar surface, Duke expressed his excitement, commenting: "Fantastic! Oh, that first foot on the lunar surface is super,
      <a href="/wiki/Flight_controller#Capsule_Communicator_.28CAPCOM.29" title="Flight controller">
       Tony
      </a>
      !"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-briar_52-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-briar-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The pair's first task of the moonwalk was to unload the Lunar Roving Vehicle, the
      <a href="/wiki/Far_Ultraviolet_Camera/Spectrograph" title="Far Ultraviolet Camera/Spectrograph">
       Far Ultraviolet Camera/Spectrograph
      </a>
      (UVC),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and other equipment, from the Lunar Module. This was done without problems. On first driving the lunar rover, Young discovered that the rear steering was not working. He alerted Mission Control to the problem before setting up the television camera and planting the
      <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Flag_Assembly" title="Lunar Flag Assembly">
       flag of the United States
      </a>
      with Duke. The day's next task was to deploy the
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Surface_Experiments_Package" title="Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Package">
       Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Package
      </a>
      (ALSEP); while they were parking the lunar rover, on which the TV camera was mounted, to observe the deployment, the rear steering began functioning without explanation. While deploying a heat-flow experiment that had burned up with the Lunar Module
      <i>
       Aquarius
      </i>
      on Apollo 13 and had been attempted without success on Apollo 15, a cable was inadvertently snapped after getting caught around Young's foot. After ALSEP deployment, they collected samples in the vicinity. About four hours after the beginning of EVA-1, they mounted the lunar rover and drove to the first geologic stop, Plum Crater, a 36m-wide (118ft) crater on the rim of Flag Crater, a crater 290m (950ft) across. There, at a distance of 1.4km (0.87mi) from the LM, they sampled material from the vicinity of Flag Crater, which scientists believed penetrated through the upper
      <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
       regolith
      </a>
      layer to the underlying Cayley Formation. It was there that Young retrieved, at the request of Mission Control, the largest rock returned by an Apollo mission, a
      <a href="/wiki/Breccia" title="Breccia">
       breccia
      </a>
      nicknamed
      <a href="/wiki/Big_Muley" title="Big Muley">
       Big Muley
      </a>
      after mission geology principal investigator
      <a href="/wiki/William_R._Muehlberger" title="William R. Muehlberger">
       William R. Muehlberger
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-honeysuckle_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-honeysuckle-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The next stop of the day was Buster Crater, about 1.6km (0.99mi) from the LM. There, Duke took pictures of Stone Mountain and South Ray Crater while Young deployed a
      <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
       magnetic field
      </a>
      experiment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At that point, scientists began to reconsider their pre-mission hypothesis that Descartes had been the setting of ancient volcanic activity, as the two astronauts had yet to find any volcanic material. Following their stop at Buster, Young did a demonstration drive of the lunar rover while Duke filmed with a
      <a href="/wiki/16_mm_film" title="16 mm film">
       16 mm
      </a>
      movie camera.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After completing more tasks at the ALSEP, they returned to the LM to close out the moonwalk. They reentered the LM 7 hours, 6 minutes, and 56 seconds after the start of the EVA. Once inside, they pressurized the LM cabin, went through a half-hour briefing with scientists in Mission Control, and configured the cabin for the sleep period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-honeysuckle_54-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-honeysuckle-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_and_Rover_on_the_Descartes_-_GPN-2000-001133.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="3022" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Young_and_Rover_on_the_Descartes_-_GPN-2000-001133.jpg/220px-Young_and_Rover_on_the_Descartes_-_GPN-2000-001133.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Young_and_Rover_on_the_Descartes_-_GPN-2000-001133.jpg/330px-Young_and_Rover_on_the_Descartes_-_GPN-2000-001133.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Young_and_Rover_on_the_Descartes_-_GPN-2000-001133.jpg/440px-Young_and_Rover_on_the_Descartes_-_GPN-2000-001133.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_and_Rover_on_the_Descartes_-_GPN-2000-001133.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The view from the side of Stone Mountain
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Shortly after waking up on the morning of flight day six three and a half minutes early, they discussed with Mission Control in Houston the day's timeline of events.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The second lunar excursion's primary objective was to visit Stone Mountain to climb up the slope of about 20 degrees to reach a cluster of five craters known as "Cinco Craters." After preparations for the day's moonwalk were completed, the astronauts climbed out of the Lunar Module. After departing the immediate landing site in the lunar rover, they arrived at the day's first destination, the Cinco Craters, 3.8km (2.4mi) from the LM. At 152m (499ft) above the valley floor, the pair were at the highest elevation above the LM of any Apollo mission. After marveling at the view (including
      <a href="/wiki/South_Ray_(crater)" title="South Ray (crater)">
       South Ray
      </a>
      ) from the side of Stone Mountain, which Duke described as "spectacular,"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the astronauts gathered samples in the vicinity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-honeysuckle_54-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-honeysuckle-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After spending 54 minutes on the slope, they climbed aboard the lunar rover en route to the day's second stop, station five, a crater 20m (66ft) across. There, they hoped to find Descartes material that had not been contaminated by ejecta from South Ray Crater, a large crater south of the landing site. The samples they collected there, although their origin is still not certain, are, according to geologist
      <a href="/wiki/Donald_Wilhelms" title="Donald Wilhelms">
       Don Wilhelms
      </a>
      , "a reasonable bet to be Descartes."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-honeysuckle_54-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-honeysuckle-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The next stop, station six, was a 10m-wide (33ft) blocky crater, where the astronauts believed they could sample the Cayley Formation as evidenced by the firmer soil found there. Bypassing station seven to save time, they arrived at station eight on the lower flank of Stone Mountain, where they sampled material on a ray from South Ray Crater for about an hour. There, they collected black and white breccias and smaller,
      <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">
       crystalline
      </a>
      rocks rich in
      <a href="/wiki/Plagioclase" title="Plagioclase">
       plagioclase
      </a>
      . At station nine, an area known as the "Vacant Lot,"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which was believed to be free of ejecta from
      <a href="/wiki/South_Ray_(crater)" title="South Ray (crater)">
       South Ray
      </a>
      , they spent about 40 minutes gathering samples. Twenty-five minutes after departing station nine, they arrived at the final stop of the day, halfway between the ALSEP site and the LM. There, they dug a double core and conducted several
      <a href="/wiki/Penetrometer" title="Penetrometer">
       penetrometer
      </a>
      tests along a line stretching 50m (160ft) east of the ALSEP. At the request of Young and Duke, the moonwalk was extended by ten minutes. After returning to the LM to wrap up the second lunar excursion, they climbed back inside the landing craft's cabin, sealing and pressurizing the interior after 7 hours, 23 minutes, and 26 seconds of EVA time, breaking a record that had been set on Apollo 15.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-honeysuckle_54-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-honeysuckle-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After eating a meal and proceeding with a debriefing on the day's activities with Mission Control, they reconfigured the LM cabin and prepared for the sleep period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charlie_Duke_near_Shadow_Rock,_Apollo_16.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2350" data-file-width="2340" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Charlie_Duke_near_Shadow_Rock%2C_Apollo_16.jpg/220px-Charlie_Duke_near_Shadow_Rock%2C_Apollo_16.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Charlie_Duke_near_Shadow_Rock%2C_Apollo_16.jpg/330px-Charlie_Duke_near_Shadow_Rock%2C_Apollo_16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Charlie_Duke_near_Shadow_Rock%2C_Apollo_16.jpg/440px-Charlie_Duke_near_Shadow_Rock%2C_Apollo_16.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charlie_Duke_near_Shadow_Rock,_Apollo_16.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Charles Duke stands in the shadow of Shadow Rock
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:House_Rock_AS16-106-17336HR.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="473" data-file-width="665" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/House_Rock_AS16-106-17336HR.jpg/220px-House_Rock_AS16-106-17336HR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/House_Rock_AS16-106-17336HR.jpg/330px-House_Rock_AS16-106-17336HR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/House_Rock_AS16-106-17336HR.jpg/440px-House_Rock_AS16-106-17336HR.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:House_Rock_AS16-106-17336HR.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        House Rock
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Flight day seven was their third and final day on the lunar surface, returning to orbit to rejoin Mattingly in the Command/Service Module following the day's moonwalk. During the third and final lunar excursion, they were to explore North Ray Crater, the largest of any of the craters any Apollo expedition had visited. After exiting
      <i>
       Orion
      </i>
      , the pair drove the lunar rover 0.8km (0.50mi) away from the LM before adjusting their heading to travel 1.4km (0.87mi) to North Ray Crater. The drive was smoother than that of the previous day, as the craters were shallower and boulders were less abundant north of the immediate landing site. Boulders gradually became larger and more abundant as they approached North Ray in the lunar rover. Upon arriving at the rim of
      <a href="/wiki/North_Ray_(crater)" title="North Ray (crater)">
       North Ray
      </a>
      crater, they were 4.4km (2.7mi) away from the LM. After their arrival, the duo took photographs of the 1km (0.62mi) wide and 230m (750ft) deep crater. They visited a large boulder, taller than a four-story building, which became known as 'House Rock'. Samples obtained from this boulder delivered the final blow to the pre-mission volcanic hypothesis, proving it incorrect. House Rock had numerous bullet hole-like marks where
      <a href="/wiki/Micrometeoroid" title="Micrometeoroid">
       micrometeoroids
      </a>
      from space had impacted the rock. About 1 hour and 22 minutes after arriving, they departed for station 13, a large boulder field about 0.5km (0.31mi) from North Ray. On the way, they set a lunar speed record, traveling at an estimated 17.1 kilometers per hour (10.6mph) downhill. They arrived at a 3m (9.8ft) high boulder, which they called 'Shadow Rock'. Here, they sampled permanently shadowed soil. During this time, Mattingly was preparing the Command/Service Module in anticipation their return approximately six hours later. After three hours and six minutes, they returned to the LM, where they completed several experiments and offloaded the rover. A short distance from the LM, Duke placed a photograph of his family and a
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">
       United States Air Force
      </a>
      commemorative medallion on the surface.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-honeysuckle_54-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-honeysuckle-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Young drove the rover to a point about 90m (300ft) east of the LM, known as the 'VIP site,' so its television camera, controlled remotely by Mission Control, could observe Apollo 16's liftoff from the Moon. They then reentered the LM after a 5 hour and 40 minute final excursion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After pressurizing the LM cabin, the crew began preparing to return to lunar orbit.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_Earth">
       Return to Earth
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_16&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Return to Earth">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Decollage_du_lem.gif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Decollage_du_lem.gif/220px-Decollage_du_lem.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Decollage_du_lem.gif/330px-Decollage_du_lem.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Decollage_du_lem.gif/440px-Decollage_du_lem.gif 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Decollage_du_lem.gif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Launch of the ascent stage of the Apollo 16 Lunar Module from the lunar surface
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Eight minutes before departing the lunar surface, CAPCOM
      <a href="/wiki/James_Irwin" title="James Irwin">
       James Irwin
      </a>
      notified Young and Duke from Mission Control that they were go for liftoff. Two minutes before launch, they activated the "Master Arm" switch and then the "Abort Stage" button, after which they awaited ignition of
      <i>
       Orion
      </i>
      s
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module#Ascent_stage" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
       ascent stage
      </a>
      engine. When the ascent stage ignited, small explosive charges severed the ascent stage from the
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module#Descent_stage" title="Apollo Lunar Module">
       descent stage
      </a>
      and cables connecting the two were severed by a
      <a href="/wiki/Guillotine" title="Guillotine">
       guillotine
      </a>
      -like mechanism. Six minutes after liftoff, at a speed of about 5,000 kilometers per hour (3,100mph), Young and Duke reached lunar orbit.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-honeysuckle_54-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-honeysuckle-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Young and Duke successfully rendezvoused and re-docked with Mattingly in the Command/Service Module. To minimize the transfer of lunar dust from the LM cabin into the CSM, Young and Duke cleaned the cabin before opening the hatch separating the two spacecraft. After opening the hatch and reuniting with Mattingly, the crew transferred the samples Young and Duke had collected on the surface into the CSM for transfer to Earth. After transfers were completed, the crew would sleep before jettisoning the empty Lunar Module ascent stage the next day, when it was to be crashed intentionally into the lunar surface.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fullsummary_37-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-fullsummary-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The next day, after final checks were completed, the expended LM ascent stage was jettisoned.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lmjett_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lmjett-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
