      Because of a failure by the crew to activate a certain switch in the LM before sealing it off, it initially tumbled after separation and did not execute the rocket burn necessary for the craft's intentional de-orbit. The ascent stage eventually crashed into the lunar surface nearly a year after the mission. The crew's next task, after jettisoning the Lunar Module ascent stage, was to release a subsatellite into lunar orbit from the CSM's Scientific Instrument Bay. The burn to alter the CSM's orbit to that desired for the subsatellite had been cancelled; as a result, the subsatellite lasted half of its anticipated lifetime. Just under five hours later, on the CSM's 65th orbit around the Moon, its Service Propulsion System main engine was reignited to propel the craft on a trajectory that would return it to Earth. The SPS engine performed the burn flawlessly despite the malfunction that had delayed the lunar landing several days before.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fullsummary_37-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-fullsummary-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lmjett_69-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-lmjett-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:S72-37001.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2141" data-file-width="3072" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/S72-37001.jpg/220px-S72-37001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/S72-37001.jpg/330px-S72-37001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/S72-37001.jpg/440px-S72-37001.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:S72-37001.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Ken Mattingly performs his deep-space EVA, retrieving film cassettes from the CSM's exterior
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      At a distance of about 170,000 nautical miles (310,000km) from Earth, Mattingly performed a "deep-space" extra-vehicular activity, or spacewalk, during which he retrieved several film cassettes from the CSM's SIM bay. While outside the spacecraft, Mattingly set up a biological experiment, the Microbial Ecology Evaluation Device (MEED).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d10_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-d10-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The MEED experiment was only performed on Apollo 16.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meed_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-meed-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The crew carried out various housekeeping and maintenance tasks aboard the spacecraft and ate a meal before concluding the day.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d10_70-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-d10-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The penultimate day of the flight was largely spent performing experiments, aside from a twenty-minute press conference during the second half of the day. During the press conference, the astronauts answered questions pertaining to several technical and non-technical aspects of the mission prepared and listed by priority at the
      <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">
       Manned Spacecraft Center
      </a>
      in Houston by journalists covering the flight. In addition to numerous housekeeping tasks, the astronauts prepared the spacecraft for its
      <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">
       atmospheric reentry
      </a>
      the next day. At the end of the crew's final full day in space, the spacecraft was approximately 77,000 nautical miles (143,000km) from Earth and closing at a rate of about 7,000 feet per second (2,100m/s).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When the wake-up call was issued to the crew for their final day in space by CAPCOM
      <a href="/wiki/Anthony_W._England" title="Anthony W. England">
       Tony England
      </a>
      , it was about 45,000 nautical miles (83,000km) out from Earth, traveling just over 9,000ft/s (2,700m/s). Just over three hours before
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Splashdown_(spacecraft_landing)" title="Splashdown (spacecraft landing)">
       splashdown
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">
       Pacific Ocean
      </a>
      , the crew performed a final course correction burn, changing their velocity by 1.4ft/s (0.43m/s). Approximately ten minutes before reentry into Earth's atmosphere, the cone-shaped Command Module containing the three crewmembers separated from the Service Module, which would burn up during reentry. At 265 hours and 37 minutes into the mission, at a velocity of about 36,000ft/s (11,000m/s), Apollo 16 began atmospheric reentry. At its maximum, the temperature of the heat shield was between 4,000 and 4,500F (2,200 and 2,480C). After successful parachute deployment and less than 14 minutes after reentry began, the Command Module
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Splashdown_(spacecraft_landing)" title="Splashdown (spacecraft landing)">
       splashed down
      </a>
      in the Pacific Ocean 350km (220mi) southeast of the island of
      <a href="/wiki/Kiritimati" title="Kiritimati">
       Kiritimati
      </a>
      (or "Christmas Island"), 290 hours, 37 minutes, 6 seconds after liftoff. The spacecraft and its crew was retrieved by the
      <a href="/wiki/USS_Ticonderoga_(CV-14)" title="USS Ticonderoga (CV-14)">
       USS
       <i>
        Ticonderoga
       </i>
      </a>
      . They were safely aboard the
      <i>
       Ticonderoga
      </i>
      37 minutes after splashdown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fullsummary_37-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-fullsummary-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_subsatellite_PFS-2">
       Lunar subsatellite PFS-2
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_16&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Lunar subsatellite PFS-2">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_15_Subsatellite.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="928" data-file-width="1200" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Apollo_15_Subsatellite.jpg/220px-Apollo_15_Subsatellite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Apollo_15_Subsatellite.jpg/330px-Apollo_15_Subsatellite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Apollo_15_Subsatellite.jpg/440px-Apollo_15_Subsatellite.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_15_Subsatellite.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Artist's conception of subsatellite deployment
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Apollo 16 subsatellite (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PFS-2" title="PFS-2">
       PFS-2
      </a>
      ) was a small satellite released into lunar orbit from the Service Module. Its principal objective was to measure charged particles and magnetic fields all around the Moon as the Moon orbited Earth, similar to its sister spacecraft,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PFS-1" title="PFS-1">
       PFS-1
      </a>
      , released eight months earlier by Apollo 15. "The low orbits of both subsatellites were to be similar ellipses, ranging from 55 to 76 miles (89 to 122 kilometres) above the lunar surface."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20061106_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nasa20061106-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      "Instead, something bizarre happened. The orbit of PFS-2 rapidly changed shape and distance from the Moon. In 2-1/2 weeks the satellite was swooping to within a hair-raising 6 miles (9.7km) of the lunar surface at closest approach. As the orbit kept changing, PFS-2 backed off again, until it seemed to be a safe 30 miles away. But not for long: inexorably, the subsatellite's orbit carried it back toward the Moon. And on May 29, 1972only 35 days and 425 orbits after its release"PFS-2 crashed into the Lunar surface.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20061106_75-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nasa20061106-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In later years, through a study of many
      <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit" title="Lunar orbit">
       lunar orbiting
      </a>
      satellites, scientists came to discover that most
      <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit" title="Lunar orbit">
       low lunar orbits
      </a>
      (LLO) are unstable. PFS-2 had been placed, unknown to mission planners at the time, squarely into one of the most unstable of orbits, at 11 degrees
      <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">
       orbital inclination
      </a>
      , far from the four
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Frozen_orbit" title="Frozen orbit">
        frozen lunar orbits
       </a>
      </i>
      discovered only later at 27, 50, 76, and 86 inclination.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20061106_75-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-nasa20061106-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_locations">
       Spacecraft locations
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Apollo_16&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Spacecraft locations">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:366px;max-width:366px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:212px;max-width:212px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_16_capsule.JPG">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="3168" data-file-width="4752" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Apollo_16_capsule.JPG/210px-Apollo_16_capsule.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Apollo_16_capsule.JPG/315px-Apollo_16_capsule.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Apollo_16_capsule.JPG/420px-Apollo_16_capsule.JPG 2x" width="210"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Command Module at the
         <a href="/wiki/U.S._Space_%26_Rocket_Center" title="U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center">
          U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:150px;max-width:150px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A16-plaque.jpg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="1820" data-file-width="1928" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/A16-plaque.jpg/148px-A16-plaque.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/A16-plaque.jpg/222px-A16-plaque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/A16-plaque.jpg/296px-A16-plaque.jpg 2x" width="148"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         The plaque left on the Moon by Apollo 16
        </div>
       </div>
       <div style="clear:left">
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The aircraft carrier USS
      <i>
       Ticonderoga
      </i>
      delivered the Apollo 16 Command Module to the
      <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_North_Island" title="Naval Air Station North Island">
       North Island Naval Air Station
      </a>
      , near San Diego, California, on Friday, May 5, 1972. On Monday, May 8, 1972, ground service equipment being used to empty the residual toxic
      <a href="/wiki/Reaction_control_system" title="Reaction control system">
       reaction control system
      </a>
      fuel in the Command Module tanks exploded in a Naval Air Station hangar. Forty-six people were sent to the hospital for 24 to 48 hours observation, most suffering from inhalation of toxic fumes. Most seriously injured was a technician who suffered a fractured kneecap when the GSE cart overturned on him. A hole was blown in the hangar roof 250 feet above; about 40 windows in the hangar were shattered. The Command Module suffered a three-inch gash in one panel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Apollo 16 Command Module
      <i>
       Casper
      </i>
      is on display at the
      <a href="/wiki/U.S._Space_%26_Rocket_Center" title="U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center">
       U.S. Space &amp; Rocket Center
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama" title="Huntsville, Alabama">
       Huntsville, Alabama
      </a>
      . The Lunar Module ascent stage separated 24 April 1972 but a loss of attitude control rendered it out of control. It orbited the Moon for about a year. Its impact site on the Moon is unknown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fullsummary_37-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-fullsummary-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Duke donated some flown items, including a lunar map, to
      <a href="/wiki/Kennesaw_State_University" title="Kennesaw State University">
       Kennesaw State University
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Kennesaw,_Georgia" title="Kennesaw, Georgia">
       Kennesaw, Georgia
      </a>
      . He left two items on the Moon, both of which he photographed. The most famous is a plastic-encased photo portrait of his family (NASA Photo AS16-117-18841
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moonfamily_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-moonfamily-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ). The reverse of the photo is signed by Duke's family and bears this message: "This is the family of Astronaut Duke from Planet Earth. Landed on the Moon, April 1972." The other item was a commemorative medal issued by the United States Air Force, which was celebrating its 25th anniversary in 1972. He took two medals, leaving one on the Moon and donating the other to the
      <a href="/wiki/Wright-Patterson_Air_Force_Base" title="Wright-Patterson Air Force Base">
       Wright-Patterson Air Force Base
      </a>
      museum.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duke1999_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Duke1999-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2006, shortly after
      <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Ernesto_(2006)" title="Hurricane Ernesto (2006)">
       Hurricane Ernesto
      </a>
      affected
      <a href="/wiki/Bath,_North_Carolina" title="Bath, North Carolina">
       Bath, North Carolina
      </a>
      , eleven-year-old Kevin Schanze discovered a piece of metal debris on the ground near his beach home. Schanze and a friend discovered a "stamp" on the 36-inch (91cm) flat metal sheet, which upon further inspection turned out to be a faded copy of the Apollo 16 mission insignia. NASA later confirmed the object to be a piece of the first stage of the Saturn V rocket that launched Apollo 16 into space. In July 2011, after returning the piece of debris at NASA's request, 16-year-old Schanze was given an all-access tour of the Kennedy Space Center and VIP seating for the launch of
      <a href="/wiki/STS-135" title="STS-135">
       STS-135
      </a>
      , the final mission of the
      <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program" title="Space Shuttle program">
       Space Shuttle program
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

       <li id="cite_note-74">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-74">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation web">
          <a class="external text" href="http://history.nasa.gov/ap16fj/30_Day12.htm#265_22_32" rel="nofollow">
           "Apollo 16: Day 12 - Entry and Splashdown"
          </a>
          . Apollo 16 Flight Journal
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            1 December
           </span>
           2011
          </span>
          .
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AApollo+16&amp;rft.btitle=Apollo+16%3A+Day+12+-+Entry+and+Splashdown&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fhistory.nasa.gov%2Fap16fj%2F30_Day12.htm%23265_22_32&amp;rft.pub=Apollo+16+Flight+Journal&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-nasa20061106-75">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-nasa20061106_75-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-nasa20061106_75-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-nasa20061106_75-2">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             c
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation web">
          Bell, Trudy E. (November 6, 2006). Phillips, Tony, ed.
          <a class="external text" href="http://science.nasa.gov/science-news/science-at-nasa/2006/06nov_loworbit/" rel="nofollow">
           "Bizarre Lunar Orbits"
          </a>
          .
          <i>
           Science@NASA
          </i>
          . NASA
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            2012-12-09
           </span>
          </span>
          .
          <q>
           <i>
            Lunar mascons make most low lunar orbits unstable ... As a satellite passes 50 or 60 miles overhead, the mascons pull it forward, back, left, right, or down, the exact direction and magnitude of the tugging depends on the satellite's trajectory. Absent any periodic boosts from onboard rockets to correct the orbit, most satellites released into low lunar orbits (under about 60 miles or 100 km) will eventually crash into the Moon. ... [There are] a number of 'frozen orbits' where a spacecraft can stay in a low lunar orbit indefinitely. They occur at four inclinations: 27, 50, 76, and 86"the last one being nearly over the lunar poles. The orbit of the relatively long-lived Apollo 15 subsatellite
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PFS-1" title="PFS-1">
             PFS-1
            </a>
            had an inclination of 28, which turned out to be close to the inclination of one of the frozen orbitsbut poor PFS-2 was cursed with an inclination of only 11.
           </i>
          </q>
         </span>

            <b>

            <b>
             bold text
            </b>

            <i>
             italics
            </i>
            . Payloads deployed from other spacecraft are denoted in (brackets).
